<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Wright brothers</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><b>Signatures</b><br/>
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orville_Wright_Signature.svg" title="Signature of Orville Wright"><img alt="Signature of Orville Wright" data-file-height="132" data-file-width="422" height="31" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Orville_Wright_Signature.svg/100px-Orville_Wright_Signature.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Orville_Wright_Signature.svg/151px-Orville_Wright_Signature.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Orville_Wright_Signature.svg/199px-Orville_Wright_Signature.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a>    <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wilbur_Wright_Signature.svg" title="Signature of Wilbur Wright"><img alt="Signature of Wilbur Wright" data-file-height="77" data-file-width="299" height="31" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Wilbur_Wright_Signature.svg/121px-Wilbur_Wright_Signature.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Wilbur_Wright_Signature.svg/183px-Wilbur_Wright_Signature.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Wilbur_Wright_Signature.svg/241px-Wilbur_Wright_Signature.svg.png 2x" width="121"/></a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>Wright brothers</b>, <b>Orville</b> (August 19, 1871 – January 30, 1948) and <b>Wilbur</b> (April 16, 1867 – May 30, 1912), were two American brothers, inventors, and aviation pioneers who are generally credited<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wb-smithson-edu_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-wb-smithson-edu-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wb-wstate-univ_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-wb-wstate-univ-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_News_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News-3">[3]</a></sup> with inventing, building, and flying the world's first successful <a href="/wiki/Airplane" title="Airplane">airplane</a>. They made the first controlled, sustained flight of a powered, heavier-than-air aircraft on December 17, 1903, four miles south of <a href="/wiki/Kitty_Hawk,_North_Carolina" title="Kitty Hawk, North Carolina">Kitty Hawk, North Carolina</a>. In 1904–05 the brothers developed their <a href="/wiki/Aircraft" title="Aircraft">flying machine</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Wright_Flyer_III" title="Wright Flyer III">first practical fixed-wing aircraft</a>. Although not the first to build and fly experimental aircraft, the Wright brothers were the first to invent aircraft controls that made fixed-wing powered flight possible.</p>
<p>The brothers' fundamental breakthrough was their invention of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flight_dynamics_(aircraft)" title="Flight dynamics (aircraft)">three-axis control</a>, which enabled the pilot to steer the aircraft effectively and to maintain its equilibrium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wb-smithson-inventing_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-wb-smithson-inventing-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wb-wagg_tail-org_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-wb-wagg_tail-org-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wb-aviation-nps_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-wb-aviation-nps-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wb-aero_journal-2003_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-wb-aero_journal-2003-7">[7]</a></sup> This method became and remains standard on fixed-wing aircraft of all kinds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> From the beginning of their aeronautical work, the Wright brothers focused on developing a reliable method of pilot control as the key to solving "the flying problem". This approach differed significantly from other experimenters of the time who put more emphasis on developing powerful engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Using a small homebuilt <a href="/wiki/Wind_tunnel" title="Wind tunnel">wind tunnel</a>, the Wrights also collected more accurate data than any before, enabling them to design and build wings and propellers that were more efficient than any before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Their first U.S. patent, 821,393, did not claim invention of a flying machine, but rather, the invention of a system of aerodynamic control that manipulated a flying machine's surfaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flying_Machine_patent_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flying_Machine_patent-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>They gained the mechanical skills essential for their success by working for years in their shop with printing presses, bicycles, motors, and other machinery. Their work with bicycles in particular influenced their belief that an unstable vehicle like a flying machine could be controlled and balanced with practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> From 1900 until their first powered flights in late 1903, they conducted extensive glider tests that also developed their skills as pilots. Their bicycle shop employee <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Taylor_(mechanic)" title="Charlie Taylor (mechanic)">Charlie Taylor</a> became an important part of the team, building their first airplane engine in close collaboration with the brothers.</p>
<p>The Wright brothers' status as inventors of the airplane has been subject to counter-claims by various parties. Much controversy persists over the many <a href="/wiki/Claims_to_the_first_powered_flight" title="Claims to the first powered flight">competing claims of early aviators</a>. Edward Roach, historian for the <a href="/wiki/Dayton_Aviation_Heritage_National_Historical_Park" title="Dayton Aviation Heritage National Historical Park">Dayton Aviation Heritage National Historical Park</a> argues that they were excellent self-taught engineers who could run a small company, but they did not have the business skills or temperament to dominate the growing aviation industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>

</li>
</ul>
</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood">Childhood</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wilbur_Wright_child.jpg"><img alt="Orville" data-file-height="546" data-file-width="340" height="321" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Wilbur_Wright_child.jpg/200px-Wilbur_Wright_child.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Wilbur_Wright_child.jpg/300px-Wilbur_Wright_child.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3e/Wilbur_Wright_child.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_Orville_Wright.jpg"><img alt="Wilbur" data-file-height="4164" data-file-width="2597" height="321" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Young_Orville_Wright.jpg/200px-Young_Orville_Wright.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Young_Orville_Wright.jpg/300px-Young_Orville_Wright.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Young_Orville_Wright.jpg/400px-Young_Orville_Wright.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">
<center>Wilbur (left) and Orville (right) as children in 1876</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Wright brothers were two of seven children born to <a href="/wiki/Milton_Wright_(bishop)" title="Milton Wright (bishop)">Milton Wright</a> (1828–1917), of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_American" title="English American">English</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dutch_American" title="Dutch American">Dutch</a> ancestry, and Susan Catherine Koerner (1831–1889), of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_American" title="German American">German</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swiss_American" title="Swiss American">Swiss</a> ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wb-dayton-online_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-wb-dayton-online-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wb-loc-papers_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-wb-loc-papers-17">[17]</a></sup> Wilbur was born near <a href="/wiki/Millville,_Henry_County,_Indiana" title="Millville, Henry County, Indiana">Millville, Indiana</a>, in 1867; Orville in <a href="/wiki/Dayton,_Ohio" title="Dayton, Ohio">Dayton, Ohio</a>, in 1871. The brothers never married. The other Wright siblings were Reuchlin (1861–1920), Lorin (1862–1939), <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Wright" title="Katharine Wright">Katharine</a> (1874–1929), and twins Otis and Ida (born 1870, died in infancy). In elementary school, Orville was given to mischief and was once expelled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The direct paternal ancestry goes back to a Samuel Wright (b. 1606 in <a href="/wiki/Essex" title="Essex">Essex</a>, England) who sailed to America and <a href="/wiki/Puritan_migration_to_New_England_(1620%E2%80%9340)" title="Puritan migration to New England (1620–40)">settled in Massachusetts in 1636</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1878 their father, who traveled often as a bishop in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_United_Brethren_in_Christ" title="Church of the United Brethren in Christ">Church of the United Brethren in Christ</a>, brought home a toy helicopter for his two younger sons. The device was based on an invention of French aeronautical pioneer <a href="/wiki/Alphonse_P%C3%A9naud" title="Alphonse Pénaud">Alphonse Pénaud</a>. Made of paper, bamboo and cork with a rubber band to twirl its rotor, it was about a foot long. Wilbur and Orville played with it until it broke, and then built their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In later years, they pointed to their experience with the toy as the spark of their interest in flying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_career_and_research">Early career and research</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WrightBrothersHome.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="486" data-file-width="580" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/WrightBrothersHome.jpg/220px-WrightBrothersHome.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/WrightBrothersHome.jpg/330px-WrightBrothersHome.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/WrightBrothersHome.jpg/440px-WrightBrothersHome.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WrightBrothersHome.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wright brothers' home at 7 Hawthorn Street, Dayton about 1900. Wilbur and Orville built the covered wrap-around porch in the 1890s.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Both brothers attended high school, but did not receive diplomas. The family's abrupt move in 1884 from <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Indiana" title="Richmond, Indiana">Richmond, Indiana</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dayton" title="Dayton">Dayton</a>, Ohio, where the family had lived during the 1870s, prevented Wilbur from receiving his diploma after finishing four years of high school. The diploma was awarded posthumously to Wilbur on April 16, 1994, which would have been his 127th birthday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In late 1885 or early 1886 Wilbur was struck in the face by a hockey stick while playing an ice-skating game with friends, resulting in the loss of his front teeth. He had been vigorous and athletic until then, and although his injuries did not appear especially severe, he became withdrawn. He had planned to attend Yale. Instead, he spent the next few years largely housebound. During this time he cared for his mother who was terminally ill with tuberculosis, read extensively in his father's library and ably assisted his father during <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_United_Brethren_in_Christ_(New_Constitution)" title="Church of the United Brethren in Christ (New Constitution)">times of controversy</a> within the Brethren Church,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> but also expressed unease over his own lack of ambition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Orville dropped out of high school after his junior year to start a printing business in 1889, having designed and built his own printing press with Wilbur's help. Wilbur joined the print shop, and in March the brothers launched a weekly newspaper, the <i>West Side News</i>. Subsequent issues listed Orville as publisher and Wilbur as editor on the masthead. In April 1890 they converted the paper to a daily, <i>The Evening Item</i>, but it lasted only four months. They then focused on commercial printing. One of their clients was Orville's friend and classmate, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Laurence_Dunbar" title="Paul Laurence Dunbar">Paul Laurence Dunbar</a>, who rose to international acclaim as a ground-breaking African-American poet and writer. For a brief period the Wrights printed the <i>Dayton Tattler</i>, a weekly newspaper that Dunbar edited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wb-nps-chapter4_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-wb-nps-chapter4-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WrightBrothersBicycle.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/WrightBrothersBicycle.JPG/220px-WrightBrothersBicycle.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/WrightBrothersBicycle.JPG/330px-WrightBrothersBicycle.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/WrightBrothersBicycle.JPG/440px-WrightBrothersBicycle.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WrightBrothersBicycle.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wright brothers' bicycle at the National Air and Space Museum</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Capitalizing on the national <a href="/wiki/Bike_boom#1890s" title="Bike boom">bicycle craze</a> (spurred by the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Safety_bicycle" title="Safety bicycle">safety bicycle</a> and its substantial advantages over the <a href="/wiki/Penny-farthing" title="Penny-farthing">penny-farthing</a> design), in December 1892 the brothers opened a repair and sales shop (the Wright Cycle Exchange, later the <a href="/wiki/Wright_Cycle_Company" title="Wright Cycle Company">Wright Cycle Company</a>) and in 1896 began manufacturing their own brand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wb-centennial-bicycle_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-wb-centennial-bicycle-26">[26]</a></sup> They used this endeavor to fund their growing interest in flight. In the early or mid-1890s they saw newspaper or magazine articles and probably photographs of the dramatic glides by <a href="/wiki/Otto_Lilienthal" title="Otto Lilienthal">Otto Lilienthal</a> in Germany. 1896 brought three important aeronautical events. In May, <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a> Secretary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samuel_Langley" title="Samuel Langley">Samuel Langley</a> successfully flew an unmanned steam-powered fixed-wing model aircraft. In mid-year, Chicago engineer and aviation authority <a href="/wiki/Octave_Chanute" title="Octave Chanute">Octave Chanute</a> brought together several men who tested various types of gliders over the sand dunes along the shore of Lake Michigan. In August, Lilienthal was killed in the plunge of his glider.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> These events lodged in the consciousness of the brothers. In May 1899 Wilbur wrote a letter<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> to the Smithsonian Institution requesting information and publications about aeronautics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Drawing on the work of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_George_Cayley" title="Sir George Cayley">Sir George Cayley</a>, Chanute, Lilienthal, <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>, and Langley, they began their mechanical aeronautical experimentation that year.</p>
<p>The Wright brothers always presented a unified image to the public, sharing equally in the credit for their invention. Biographers note that Wilbur took the initiative in 1899–1900, writing of "my" machine and "my" plans before Orville became deeply involved when the first person singular became the plural "we" and "our". Author James Tobin asserts, "it is impossible to imagine Orville, bright as he was, supplying the driving force that started their work and kept it going from the back room of a store in Ohio to conferences with capitalists, presidents, and kings. Will did that. He was the leader, from the beginning to the end."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideas_about_control">Ideas about control</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WrightBrothers1899Kite.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="364" data-file-width="364" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/WrightBrothers1899Kite.jpg/220px-WrightBrothers1899Kite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/WrightBrothers1899Kite.jpg/330px-WrightBrothers1899Kite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e6/WrightBrothers1899Kite.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WrightBrothers1899Kite.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wright 1899 kite: front and side views, with control sticks. Wing-warping is shown in lower view. (Wright brothers drawing in Library of Congress)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite Lilienthal's fate, the brothers favored his strategy: to practice gliding in order to master the art of control before attempting motor-driven flight. The death of British aeronaut <a href="/wiki/Percy_Pilcher" title="Percy Pilcher">Percy Pilcher</a> in another hang gliding crash in October 1899 only reinforced their opinion that a reliable method of pilot control was the key to successful—and safe—flight. At the outset of their experiments they regarded control as the unsolved third part of "the flying problem". They believed sufficiently promising knowledge of the other two issues—wings and engines—already existed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The Wright brothers thus differed sharply from more experienced practitioners of the day, notably <a href="/wiki/Cl%C3%A9ment_Ader" title="Clément Ader">Clément Ader</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hiram_Maxim" title="Hiram Maxim">Maxim</a> and Langley who built powerful engines, attached them to airframes equipped with unproven control devices, and expected to take to the air with no previous flying experience. Although agreeing with Lilienthal's idea of practice, the Wrights saw that his method of balance and control by shifting his body weight was inadequate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> They were determined to find something better.</p>
<p>On the basis of observation, Wilbur concluded that birds changed the angle of the ends of their wings to make their bodies roll right or left.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The brothers decided this would also be a good way for a flying machine to turn—to "bank" or "lean" into the turn just like a bird—and just like a person riding a bicycle, an experience with which they were thoroughly familiar. Equally important, they hoped this method would enable recovery when the wind tilted the machine to one side (lateral balance). They puzzled over how to achieve the same effect with man-made wings and eventually discovered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wing-warping" title="Wing-warping">wing-warping</a> when Wilbur idly twisted a long inner-tube box at the bicycle shop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other aeronautical investigators regarded flight as if it were not so different from surface locomotion, except the surface would be elevated. They thought in terms of a ship's rudder for steering, while the flying machine remained essentially level in the air, as did a train or an automobile or a ship at the surface. The idea of deliberately leaning, or rolling, to one side seemed either undesirable or did not enter their thinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Some of these other investigators, including Langley and Chanute, sought the elusive ideal of "inherent stability", believing the pilot of a flying machine would not be able to react quickly enough to wind disturbances to use mechanical controls effectively. The Wright brothers, on the other hand, wanted the pilot to have absolute control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> For that reason, their early designs made no concessions toward built-in stability (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dihedral_(aircraft)" title="Dihedral (aircraft)">dihedral</a> wings). They deliberately designed their 1903 first powered flyer with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dihedral_(aircraft)#Anhedral" title="Dihedral (aircraft)">anhedral</a> (drooping) wings, which are inherently unstable, but less susceptible to upset by gusty cross winds.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Flights">Flights</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toward_flight">Toward flight</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Park_Ranker_Wright_Brothers_Memorial.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="534" data-file-width="800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Park_Ranker_Wright_Brothers_Memorial.JPG/220px-Park_Ranker_Wright_Brothers_Memorial.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Park_Ranker_Wright_Brothers_Memorial.JPG/330px-Park_Ranker_Wright_Brothers_Memorial.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Park_Ranker_Wright_Brothers_Memorial.JPG/440px-Park_Ranker_Wright_Brothers_Memorial.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Park_Ranker_Wright_Brothers_Memorial.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Park Ranger Tom White demonstrates a replica of the Wright brothers 1899 box kite at the <a href="/wiki/Wright_Brothers_National_Memorial" title="Wright Brothers National Memorial">Wright Brothers National Memorial</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In July 1899 Wilbur put <a href="/wiki/Wing_warping" title="Wing warping">wing warping</a> to the test by building and flying a biplane kite with a five-foot (1.5m) wingspan. When the wings were warped, or twisted, one end of the wings produced more lift and the other end less lift. The unequal lift made the wings tilt, or bank: the end with more lift rose, while the other end dropped, causing a turn in the direction of the lower end. The warping was controlled by four cords attached to the kite, which led to two sticks held by the kite flyer, who tilted them in opposite directions to twist the wings.</p>
<p>In 1900 the brothers went to <a href="/wiki/Kitty_Hawk,_North_Carolina" title="Kitty Hawk, North Carolina">Kitty Hawk</a>, North Carolina, to begin their manned gliding experiments. In his reply to Wilbur's first letter, Octave Chanute had suggested the mid-Atlantic coast for its regular breezes and soft sandy landing surface. Wilbur also requested and examined U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Service" title="National Weather Service">Weather Bureau</a> data, and decided on Kitty Hawk after receiving information from the government meteorologist stationed there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WDL_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-WDL-37">[37]</a></sup> Kitty Hawk, although remote, was closer to Dayton than other places Chanute had suggested, including California and Florida. The spot also gave them privacy from reporters, who had turned the 1896 Chanute experiments at Lake Michigan into something of a circus. Chanute visited them in camp each season from 1901 to 1903 and saw gliding experiments, but not the powered flights.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gliders">Gliders</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wright_Glider" title="Wright Glider">Wright Glider</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chanute-Herring_1896_hang_glider.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="223" data-file-width="313" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Chanute-Herring_1896_hang_glider.jpg/220px-Chanute-Herring_1896_hang_glider.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/Chanute-Herring_1896_hang_glider.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chanute-Herring_1896_hang_glider.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Chanute's hang glider of 1896. The pilot may be <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Moore_Herring" title="Augustus Moore Herring">Augustus Herring.</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Wrights based the design of their kite and full-size gliders on work done in the 1890s by other aviation pioneers. They adopted the basic design of the Chanute-Herring biplane hang glider ("double-decker" as the Wrights called it), which flew well in the 1896 experiments near Chicago, and used aeronautical data on <a href="/wiki/Lift_(force)" title="Lift (force)">lift</a> that Lilienthal had published. The Wrights designed the wings with <a href="/wiki/Camber_(aerodynamics)" title="Camber (aerodynamics)">camber</a>, a curvature of the top surface. The brothers did not discover this principle, but took advantage of it. The better lift of a cambered surface compared to a flat one was first discussed scientifically by <a href="/wiki/George_Cayley" title="George Cayley">Sir George Cayley</a>. Lilienthal, whose work the Wrights carefully studied, used cambered wings in his gliders, proving in flight the advantage over flat surfaces. The wooden uprights between the wings of the Wright glider were braced by wires in their own version of Chanute's modified <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pratt_truss" title="Pratt truss">Pratt truss</a>, a bridge-building design he used for his biplane glider (initially built as a triplane). The Wrights mounted the horizontal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elevator_(aircraft)" title="Elevator (aircraft)">elevator</a> in front of the wings rather than behind, apparently believing this feature would help to avoid, or protect them, from a nosedive and crash like the one that killed Lilienthal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Wilbur incorrectly believed a tail was not necessary,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and their first two gliders did not have one. According to some Wright biographers, Wilbur probably did all the gliding until 1902, perhaps to exercise his authority as older brother and to protect Orville from harm as he did not want to have to explain to Bishop Wright if Orville got injured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable">

</tr>

<p>* (This airfoil caused severe stability problems; the Wrights modified the camber on-site.)</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1900">1900</span></h4>
<p>The brothers flew the glider for only a few days in the early autumn of 1900 at Kitty Hawk. In the first tests, probably on October 3, Wilbur was aboard while the glider flew as a kite not far above the ground with men below holding tether ropes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Most of the kite tests were unpiloted, with sandbags or chains and even a local boy as ballast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WrightBrothers1900Glider.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3196" data-file-width="3896" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/WrightBrothers1900Glider.jpg/220px-WrightBrothers1900Glider.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/WrightBrothers1900Glider.jpg/330px-WrightBrothers1900Glider.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/WrightBrothers1900Glider.jpg/440px-WrightBrothers1900Glider.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WrightBrothers1900Glider.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 1900 glider. No photo was taken with a pilot aboard.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>They tested wing-warping using control ropes from the ground. The glider was also tested unmanned while suspended from a small homemade tower. Wilbur, but not Orville, made about a dozen free glides on only a single day, October 20. For those tests the brothers trekked four miles (6 km) south to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kill_Devil_Hills" title="Kill Devil Hills">Kill Devil Hills</a>, a group of sand dunes up to 100 feet (30 m) high (where they made camp in each of the next three years). Although the glider's lift was less than expected, the brothers were encouraged because the craft's front elevator worked well and they had no accidents. However, the small number of free glides meant they were not able to give wing-warping a true test.</p>
<p>The pilot lay flat on the lower wing, as planned, to reduce aerodynamic drag. As a glide ended, the pilot was supposed to lower himself to a vertical position through an opening in the wing and land on his feet with his arms wrapped over the framework. Within a few glides, however, they discovered the pilot could remain prone on the wing, headfirst, without undue danger when landing. They made all their flights in that position for the next five years.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1901">1901</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wright1901GliderBottom.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2002" data-file-width="3581" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wright1901GliderBottom.jpg/220px-Wright1901GliderBottom.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wright1901GliderBottom.jpg/330px-Wright1901GliderBottom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wright1901GliderBottom.jpg/440px-Wright1901GliderBottom.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wright1901GliderBottom.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Orville with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1901_Wright_Glider" title="1901 Wright Glider">1901 glider</a>, its nose pointed skyward; it had no tail.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hoping to improve lift, they built the 1901 glider with a much larger wing area and made dozens of flights in July and August for distances of 50 to 400 ft (15 to 122 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The glider stalled a few times, but the parachute effect of the forward elevator allowed Wilbur to make a safe flat landing, instead of a nose-dive. These incidents wedded the Wrights even more strongly to the <i><a href="/wiki/Canard_(aeronautics)" title="Canard (aeronautics)">canard</a></i> design, which they did not give up until 1910. The glider, however, delivered two major disappointments. It produced only about one-third the lift calculated and sometimes pointed opposite the intended direction of a turn–a problem later known as <a href="/wiki/Adverse_yaw" title="Adverse yaw">adverse yaw</a>–when Wilbur used the wing-warping control. On the trip home a deeply dejected Wilbur remarked to Orville that man would not fly in a thousand years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wright_1901_glider_landing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3464" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Wright_1901_glider_landing.jpg/220px-Wright_1901_glider_landing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Wright_1901_glider_landing.jpg/330px-Wright_1901_glider_landing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Wright_1901_glider_landing.jpg/440px-Wright_1901_glider_landing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wright_1901_glider_landing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wilbur just after landing the 1901 glider. Glider skid marks are visible behind it, and marks from a previous landing are seen in front; Kill Devil Hills, North Carolina.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The poor lift of the gliders led the Wrights to question the accuracy of Lilienthal's data, as well as the "<a href="/wiki/John_Smeaton" title="John Smeaton">Smeaton</a> coefficient" of air pressure, a value which had been in use for over 100 years and was part of the accepted equation for lift.</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>The lift equation</caption>
<tr>
<td><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mo>=</mo>
<mi>k</mi>
<mspace width="thickmathspace"></mspace>
<mi>S</mi>
<mspace width="thickmathspace"></mspace>
<msup>
<mi>V</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mspace width="thickmathspace"></mspace>
<msub>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>L</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle L=k\;S\;V^{2}\;C_{L}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="L=k\;S\;V^{2}\;C_{L}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1a7ebf8bf4ae5dcaed40b3fdf02337ac975eca68" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:15.389ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span>
<p>L = lift in pounds<br/>
k = coefficient of air pressure (Smeaton coefficient)<br/>
S = total area of lifting surface in square feet<br/>
V = velocity (headwind plus ground speed) in miles per hour<br/>
C<sub>L</sub> = coefficient of lift (varies with wing shape)</p>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The Wrights used this equation to calculate the amount of lift that a wing would produce. Over the years a wide variety of values had been measured for the Smeaton coefficient; Chanute identified up to 50 of them. Wilbur knew that Langley, for example, had used a lower number than the traditional one. Intent on confirming the correct Smeaton value, Wilbur performed his own calculations using measurements collected during kite and free flights of the 1901 glider. His results correctly showed that the coefficient was very close to 0.0033 (similar to the number Langley used), not the traditional 0.0054, which would significantly exaggerate predicted lift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WB_Wind_Tunnel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/WB_Wind_Tunnel.jpg/220px-WB_Wind_Tunnel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/WB_Wind_Tunnel.jpg/330px-WB_Wind_Tunnel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/WB_Wind_Tunnel.jpg/440px-WB_Wind_Tunnel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WB_Wind_Tunnel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Replica of the Wright brothers' <a href="/wiki/Wind_tunnel" title="Wind tunnel">wind tunnel</a> at the Virginia Air and Space Center</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>To learn whether errors actually existed in Lilienthal's data tables, the brothers used a bicycle for a new type of experiment. They made a model-size airfoil and a counter-acting flat plate, both according to dimensions Lilienthal had specified, and attached them to an extra bicycle wheel, which they mounted horizontally in front of the handlebars. Pedaling strenuously on a local street to create airflow over the apparatus, they observed that the third wheel rotated against the airfoil instead of remaining motionless as Lilienthal's formula predicted. The experiment confirmed their suspicion that either the standard Smeaton coefficient or Lilienthal's coefficients of lift and drag–or all of them–were in error.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KittyHawkBox_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-KittyHawkBox-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>They then built a six-foot (1.8m) wind tunnel in their shop and between October and December 1901 conducted systematic tests on dozens of miniature wings .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodson_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dodson-50">[50]</a></sup> The "balances" they devised and mounted inside the tunnel to hold the wings looked crude, made of bicycle spokes and scrap metal, but were "as critical to the ultimate success of the Wright brothers as were the gliders."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The devices allowed the brothers to balance lift against drag and accurately calculate the performance of each wing. They could also see which wings worked well as they looked through the viewing window in the top of the tunnel. The tests yielded a trove of valuable data never before known and showed that the poor lift of the 1900 and 1901 gliders was entirely due to an incorrect Smeaton value, and that Lilienthal's published data were fairly accurate for the tests he had done. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KittyHawkBox_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-KittyHawkBox-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Before the detailed wind tunnel tests Wilbur traveled to <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> at Chanute's invitation to give a lecture to the <a href="/wiki/Western_Society_of_Engineers" title="Western Society of Engineers">Western Society of Engineers</a> on September 18, 1901. He presented a thorough report about the 1900–01 glider experiments and complemented his talk with a <a href="/wiki/Magic_lantern" title="Magic lantern">lantern</a> slide show of photographs. Wilbur's speech was the first public account of the brothers' experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1902">1902</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WrightGlidersSideBySide.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="1028" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/WrightGlidersSideBySide.jpg/220px-WrightGlidersSideBySide.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/WrightGlidersSideBySide.jpg/330px-WrightGlidersSideBySide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/WrightGlidersSideBySide.jpg/440px-WrightGlidersSideBySide.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WrightGlidersSideBySide.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A big improvement<br/>
At left, 1901 glider flown by Wilbur (left) and Orville. At right, 1902 glider flown by Wilbur (right) and Dan Tate, their helper. Dramatic improvement in performance is apparent. The 1901 glider flies at a steep <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_attack" title="Angle of attack">angle of attack</a> due to poor lift and high drag. In contrast, the 1902 glider flies at a much flatter angle and holds up its tether lines almost vertically, clearly demonstrating a much better <a href="/wiki/Lift-to-drag_ratio" title="Lift-to-drag ratio">lift-to-drag ratio</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Lilienthal had made "whirling arm" tests on only a few wing shapes, and the Wrights mistakenly assumed the data would apply to their wings, which had a different shape. The Wrights took a huge step forward and made basic wind tunnel tests on 200 wings of many shapes and <a href="/wiki/Airfoil" title="Airfoil">airfoil</a> curves, followed by detailed tests on 38 of them. The tests, according to biographer Fred Howard, "were the most crucial and fruitful aeronautical experiments ever conducted in so short a time with so few materials and at so little expense".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> An important discovery was the benefit of longer narrower wings: in aeronautical terms, wings with a larger <a href="/wiki/Aspect_ratio" title="Aspect ratio">aspect ratio</a> (wingspan divided by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chord_(aircraft)" title="Chord (aircraft)">chord</a>—the wing's front-to-back dimension). Such shapes offered much better <a href="/wiki/Lift-to-drag_ratio" title="Lift-to-drag ratio">lift-to-drag ratio</a> than the broader wings the brothers had tried so far.</p>
<p>With this knowledge, and a more accurate Smeaton number, the Wrights designed their 1902 glider. Using another crucial discovery from the wind tunnel, they made the airfoil flatter, reducing the camber (the depth of the wing's curvature divided by its chord). The 1901 wings had significantly greater curvature, a highly inefficient feature the Wrights copied directly from Lilienthal. Fully confident in their new wind tunnel results, the Wrights discarded Lilienthal's data, now basing their designs on their own calculations.</p>
<p>With characteristic caution, the brothers first flew the 1902 glider as an unmanned kite, as they had done with their two previous versions. Rewarding their wind tunnel work, the glider produced the expected lift. It also had a new structural feature: a fixed, rear vertical rudder, which the brothers hoped would eliminate turning problems.</p>
<p>By 1902 they realized that wing-warping created "differential drag" at the wingtips. Greater lift at one end of the wing also increased drag, which slowed that end of the wing, making the glider swivel—or "yaw"—so the nose pointed away from the turn. That was how the tailless 1901 glider behaved.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1902_WrightBrosGlider.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="522" data-file-width="438" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/1902_WrightBrosGlider.jpg/170px-1902_WrightBrosGlider.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/1902_WrightBrosGlider.jpg/255px-1902_WrightBrosGlider.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/1902_WrightBrosGlider.jpg/340px-1902_WrightBrosGlider.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1902_WrightBrosGlider.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wilbur Wright pilots the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1902_Wright_Glider" title="1902 Wright Glider">1902 glider</a> over the Kill Devil Hills, October 10, 1902. The single rear rudder is steerable; it replaced the original fixed double rudder.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The improved wing design enabled consistently longer glides, and the rear rudder prevented adverse yaw—so effectively that it introduced a new problem. Sometimes when the pilot attempted to level off from a turn, the glider failed to respond to corrective wing-warping and persisted into a tighter turn. The glider would slide toward the lower wing, which hit the ground, spinning the aircraft around. The Wrights called this "well digging".</p>
<p>Orville apparently visualized that the fixed rudder resisted the effect of corrective wing-warping when attempting to level off from a turn. He wrote in his diary that on the night of October 2, "I studied out a new vertical rudder". The brothers then decided to make the rear rudder movable to solve the problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> They hinged the rudder and connected it to the pilot's warping "cradle", so a single movement by the pilot simultaneously controlled wing-warping and rudder deflection. Tests while gliding proved that the trailing edge of the rudder should be turned away from whichever end of the wings had more drag (and lift) due to warping. The opposing pressure produced by turning the rudder enabled corrective wing-warping to reliably restore level flight after a turn or a wind disturbance. Furthermore, when the glider banked into a turn, rudder pressure overcame the effect of differential drag and pointed the nose of the aircraft in the direction of the turn, eliminating adverse yaw.</p>
<p>In short, the Wrights discovered the true purpose of the movable vertical rudder. Its role was not to change the direction of flight (as a rudder does in sailing), but rather, to aim or align the aircraft correctly during banking turns and when leveling off from turns and wind disturbances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The actual turn—the change in direction—was done with roll control using wing-warping. The principles remained the same when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ailerons" title="Ailerons">ailerons</a> superseded wing-warping.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1902_Wright_glider_turns.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2267" data-file-width="3374" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/1902_Wright_glider_turns.jpeg/220px-1902_Wright_glider_turns.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/1902_Wright_glider_turns.jpeg/330px-1902_Wright_glider_turns.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/1902_Wright_glider_turns.jpeg/440px-1902_Wright_glider_turns.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1902_Wright_glider_turns.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wilbur makes a turn using wing-warping and the movable rudder, October 24, 1902.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With their new method the Wrights achieved true control in turns for the first time on October 8, 1902, a major milestone. From September 19 to October 24 they made between 700 and 1,000 glides, the longest lasting 26 seconds and covering 622.5 feet (189.7 m). Hundreds of well-controlled glides after they made the rudder steerable convinced them they were ready to build a powered flying machine.</p>
<p>Thus did <a href="/wiki/Flight_dynamics" title="Flight dynamics">three-axis control</a> evolve: wing-warping for roll (lateral motion), forward elevator for pitch (up and down) and rear rudder for yaw (side to side). On March 23, 1903, the Wrights applied for their famous patent for a "Flying Machine", based on their successful 1902 glider. Some aviation historians believe that applying the system of three-axis flight control on the 1902 glider was equal to, or even more significant, than the addition of power to the 1903 Flyer. Peter Jakab of the Smithsonian asserts that perfection of the 1902 glider essentially represents invention of the airplane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adding_power">Adding power</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_flight2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4280" data-file-width="6603" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/First_flight2.jpg/220px-First_flight2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/First_flight2.jpg/330px-First_flight2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/First_flight2.jpg/440px-First_flight2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_flight2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
First flight of the <a href="/wiki/Wright_Flyer" title="Wright Flyer">Wright Flyer</a> I, December 17, 1903, Orville piloting, Wilbur running at wingtip.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1903 the brothers built the powered Wright Flyer I, using their preferred material for construction, <a href="/wiki/Spruce" title="Spruce">spruce</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> a strong and lightweight wood, and Pride of the West <a href="/wiki/Muslin" title="Muslin">muslin</a> for surface coverings. They also designed and carved their own wooden propellers, and had a purpose-built gasoline engine fabricated in their bicycle shop. They thought propeller design would be a simple matter and intended to adapt data from shipbuilding. However, their library research disclosed no established formulae for either marine or air propellers, and they found themselves with no sure starting point. They discussed and argued the question, sometimes heatedly, until they concluded that an aeronautical propeller is essentially a wing rotating in the vertical plane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> On that basis, they used data from more wind tunnel tests to design their propellers. The finished blades were just over eight feet long, made of three laminations of glued spruce. The Wrights decided on twin "<a href="/wiki/Pusher_configuration" title="Pusher configuration">pusher</a>" propellers (counter-rotating to cancel torque), which would act on a greater quantity of air than a single relatively slow propeller and not disturb airflow over the leading edge of the wings.</p>
<p>Wilbur made a March 1903 entry in his notebook indicating the prototype propeller was 66% efficient. Modern wind tunnel tests on reproduction 1903 propellers show they were more than 75% efficient under the conditions of the first flights, "a remarkable feat", and actually had a peak efficiency of 82%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wright_brothers_engine_17.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1136" data-file-width="852" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Wright_brothers_engine_17.jpg/170px-Wright_brothers_engine_17.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Wright_brothers_engine_17.jpg/255px-Wright_brothers_engine_17.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Wright_brothers_engine_17.jpg/340px-Wright_brothers_engine_17.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wright_brothers_engine_17.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Wright engine, serial number 17, circa 1910, on display at the <a href="/wiki/New_England_Air_Museum" title="New England Air Museum">New England Air Museum</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Wrights wrote to several engine manufacturers, but none met their need for a sufficiently lightweight powerplant. They turned to their shop mechanic, <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Taylor_(mechanic)" title="Charlie Taylor (mechanic)">Charlie Taylor</a>, who built an engine in just six weeks in close consultation with the brothers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> To keep the weight low enough, the engine block was cast from aluminum, a rare practice for the time. The Wright/Taylor engine had a primitive version of a <a href="/wiki/Carburetor" title="Carburetor">carburetor</a>, and had no <a href="/wiki/Fuel_pump" title="Fuel pump">fuel pump</a>. Gasoline was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravity</a>-fed from the fuel tank mounted on a wing strut into a chamber next to the cylinders where it was mixed with air: the fuel-air mixture was then vaporized by heat from the crankcase, forcing it into the cylinders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>The propeller <a href="/wiki/Roller_chain" title="Roller chain">drive chains</a>, resembling those of bicycles, were supplied by a manufacturer of heavy-duty automobile chains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The <i>Flyer</i> cost less than a thousand dollars, in contrast to more than $50,000 in government funds given to <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Pierpont_Langley" title="Samuel Pierpont Langley">Samuel Langley</a> for his man-carrying <a href="/wiki/Langley_Aerodrome" title="Langley Aerodrome">Great Aerodrome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The Flyer had a wingspan of 40.3 ft (12.3 m), weighed 605 lb (274 kg)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> and had a 12 horsepower (8.9 kW) 180 lb (82 kg) engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_powered_flight">First powered flight</span></h3>
<p>In camp at Kill Devil Hills, they endured weeks of delays caused by broken propeller shafts during engine tests. After the shafts were replaced (requiring two trips back to Dayton), Wilbur won a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tossing_a_coin" title="Tossing a coin">coin toss</a> and made a three-second flight attempt on December 14, 1903, stalling after takeoff and causing minor damage to the Flyer. (Because December 13, 1903, was a Sunday, the brothers did not make any attempts that day, even though the weather was good.) In a message to their family, Wilbur referred to the trial as having "only partial success", stating "the power is ample, and but for a trifling error due to lack of experience with this machine and this method of starting, the machine would undoubtedly have flown beautifully."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Following repairs, the Wrights finally took to the air on December 17, 1903, making two flights each from level ground into a freezing headwind gusting to 27 miles per hour (43 km/h). The first flight, by Orville at 10:35 am, of 120 feet (37 m) in 12 seconds, at a speed of only 6.8 miles per hour (10.9 km/h) over the ground, was recorded in a <a href="/wiki/File:First_flight2.jpg" title="File:First flight2.jpg">famous photograph</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WDL_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-WDL-37">[37]</a></sup> The next two flights covered approximately 175 and 200 feet (53 and 61 m), by Wilbur and Orville respectively. Their altitude was about 10 feet (3.0 m) above the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wb-first-five-flights_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-wb-first-five-flights-70">[70]</a></sup> The following is Orville Wright's account of the final flight of the day:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Wilbur started the fourth and last flight at just about 12 o'clock. The first few hundred feet were up and down, as before, but by the time three hundred ft had been covered, the machine was under much better control. The course for the next four or five hundred feet had but little undulation. However, when out about eight hundred feet the machine began pitching again, and, in one of its darts downward, struck the ground. The distance over the ground was measured to be 852 feet; the time of the flight was 59 seconds. The frame supporting the front rudder was badly broken, but the main part of the machine was not injured at all. We estimated that the machine could be put in condition for flight again in about a day or two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wright_diary1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1095" data-file-width="871" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Wright_diary1.jpg/220px-Wright_diary1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Wright_diary1.jpg/330px-Wright_diary1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Wright_diary1.jpg/440px-Wright_diary1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wright_diary1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Orville's notebook entry of December 17, 1903</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Five people witnessed the flights: Adam Etheridge, <a href="/wiki/John_T._Daniels" title="John T. Daniels">John T. Daniels</a> (who snapped the famous "first flight" photo using Orville's pre-positioned camera) and Will Dough, all of the U.S. government coastal lifesaving crew; area businessman W.C. Brinkley; and Johnny Moore, a teenaged boy who lived in the area. After the men hauled the Flyer back from its fourth flight, a powerful gust of wind flipped it over several times, despite the crew's attempt to hold it down. Severely damaged, the airplane never flew again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The brothers shipped it home, and years later Orville restored it, lending it to several U.S. locations for display, then to a British museum (see Smithsonian dispute below), before it was finally installed in 1948 in the Smithsonian Institution in Washington, D.C., its current residence.</p>
<p>The Wrights sent a telegram about the flights to their father, requesting that he "inform press."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WDL_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-WDL-37">[37]</a></sup> However, the <i>Dayton Journal</i> refused to publish the story, saying the flights were too short to be important. Meanwhile, against the brothers' wishes, a telegraph operator leaked their message to a Virginia newspaper, which concocted a highly inaccurate news article that was reprinted the next day in several newspapers elsewhere, including Dayton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Wrights issued their own factual statement to the press in January.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Nevertheless, the flights did not create public excitement—if people even knew about them—and the news soon faded.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In Paris, however, Aero Club of France members, already stimulated by Chanute's reports of Wright gliding successes, took the news more seriously and increased their efforts to catch up to the brothers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Modern analysis by Professor Fred E. C. Culick and Henry R. Jex (in 1985) has demonstrated that the 1903 Wright Flyer was so unstable as to be almost unmanageable by anyone but the Wrights, who had trained themselves in the 1902 glider.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trouble_establishing_legitimacy">Trouble establishing legitimacy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1904WrightFlyer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="302" data-file-width="653" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/1904WrightFlyer.jpg/220px-1904WrightFlyer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/1904WrightFlyer.jpg/330px-1904WrightFlyer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/1904WrightFlyer.jpg/440px-1904WrightFlyer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1904WrightFlyer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Orville in flight over Huffman Prairie in <a href="/wiki/Wright_Flyer_II" title="Wright Flyer II">Wright Flyer II</a>. Flight #85, approximately 1,760 feet (536 m) in <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap">40<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>5</sub></span> seconds, November 16, 1904.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1904 the Wrights built the <i>Flyer II</i>. They decided to avoid the expense of travel and bringing supplies to the Outer Banks and set up an airfield at <a href="/wiki/Huffman_Prairie" title="Huffman Prairie">Huffman Prairie</a>, a cow pasture eight miles (13 km) northeast of Dayton. They received permission to use the field rent-free from owner and bank president Torrance Huffman. They invited reporters to their first flight attempt of the year on May 23, on the condition that no photographs be taken. Engine troubles and slack winds prevented any flying, and they could manage only a very short hop a few days later with fewer reporters present. Library of Congress historian Fred Howard noted some speculation that the brothers may have intentionally failed to fly in order to cause reporters to lose interest in their experiments. Whether that is true is not known, but after their poor showing local newspapers virtually ignored them for the next year and a half.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Wrights were glad to be free from the distraction of reporters. The absence of newsmen also reduced the chance of competitors learning their methods. After the Kitty Hawk powered flights, the Wrights made a decision to begin withdrawing from the bicycle business so they could concentrate on creating and marketing a practical airplane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> This was financially risky, since they were neither wealthy nor government-funded (unlike other experimenters such as Ader, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hiram_Stevens_Maxim" title="Hiram Stevens Maxim">Maxim</a>, Langley and <a href="/wiki/Alberto_Santos-Dumont" title="Alberto Santos-Dumont">Alberto Santos-Dumont</a>). The Wright brothers did not have the luxury of being able to give away their invention; it was to be their livelihood. Thus, their secrecy intensified, encouraged by advice from their patent attorney, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harry_Aubrey_Toulmin,_Sr." title="Harry Aubrey Toulmin, Sr.">Henry Toulmin</a>, not to reveal details of their machine.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WrightFlyer1904Circling.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="327" data-file-width="426" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/WrightFlyer1904Circling.jpg/220px-WrightFlyer1904Circling.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/WrightFlyer1904Circling.jpg/330px-WrightFlyer1904Circling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/08/WrightFlyer1904Circling.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WrightFlyer1904Circling.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wilbur flying almost four circles of Huffman Prairie, about <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap">2<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>3</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span> miles in 5 minutes 4 seconds; flight #82, November 9, 1904.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WrightBrothersFirstCircleFlightLogBook.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1101" data-file-width="902" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/WrightBrothersFirstCircleFlightLogBook.gif/220px-WrightBrothersFirstCircleFlightLogBook.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/WrightBrothersFirstCircleFlightLogBook.gif/330px-WrightBrothersFirstCircleFlightLogBook.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/WrightBrothersFirstCircleFlightLogBook.gif/440px-WrightBrothersFirstCircleFlightLogBook.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WrightBrothersFirstCircleFlightLogBook.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wilbur's logbook showing diagram and data for first circle flight on September 20, 1904</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At Huffman Prairie, lighter winds made takeoffs harder, and they had to use a longer starting rail than the 60-foot (18 m) rail used at Kitty Hawk. The first flights in 1904 revealed problems with longitudinal stability, solved by adding ballast and lengthening the supports for the elevator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> During the spring and summer they suffered many hard landings, often damaging the aircraft and causing minor injuries. On August 13, making an unassisted takeoff, Wilbur finally exceeded their best Kitty Hawk effort with a flight of 1,300 feet (400 m). Then they decided to use a weight-powered catapult to make takeoffs easier and tried it for the first time on September 7. On September 20, 1904, Wilbur flew the first complete circle in history by a manned heavier-than-air powered machine, covering 4,080 feet (1,244 m) in about a minute and a half.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Their two best flights were November 9 by Wilbur and December 1 by Orville, each exceeding five minutes and covering nearly three miles in almost four circles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> By the end of the year the brothers had accumulated about 50 minutes in the air in 105 flights over the rather soggy 85 acres (34 ha) pasture, which, remarkably, is virtually unchanged today from its original condition and is now part of <a href="/wiki/Dayton_Aviation_Heritage_National_Historical_Park" title="Dayton Aviation Heritage National Historical Park">Dayton Aviation Heritage National Historical Park</a>, adjacent to <a href="/wiki/Wright-Patterson_Air_Force_Base" title="Wright-Patterson Air Force Base">Wright-Patterson Air Force Base</a>.</p>
<p>The Wrights scrapped the battered and much-repaired aircraft, but saved the engine, and in 1905 built a new airplane, the <i>Flyer III</i>. Nevertheless, at first this Flyer offered the same marginal performance as the first two. Its maiden flight was on June 23 and the first few flights were no longer than 10 seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winchester_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Winchester-83">[83]</a></sup> After Orville suffered a bone-jarring and potentially fatal crash on July 14, they rebuilt the Flyer with the forward elevator and rear rudder both enlarged and placed several feet farther away from the wings. They also installed a separate control for the rear rudder instead of linking it to the wing-warping "cradle" as before. Each of the three axes—pitch, roll and yaw—now had its own independent control. These modifications greatly improved stability and control, enabling a series of six dramatic "long flights" ranging from 17 to 38 minutes and 11 to 24 miles (39 km) around the three-quarter mile course over Huffman Prairie between September 26 and October 5. Wilbur made the last and longest flight, 24.5 miles (39.4 km) in 38 minutes and 3 seconds, ending with a safe landing when the fuel ran out. The flight was seen by a number of people, including several invited friends, their father Milton, and neighboring farmers. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wright_Flyer_III_above.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="400" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Wright_Flyer_III_above.jpg/170px-Wright_Flyer_III_above.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Wright_Flyer_III_above.jpg/255px-Wright_Flyer_III_above.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Wright_Flyer_III_above.jpg/340px-Wright_Flyer_III_above.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wright_Flyer_III_above.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Wright_Flyer_III" title="Wright Flyer III">Wright Flyer III</a> piloted by Orville over Huffman Prairie, October 4, 1905. Flight #46, covering <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap">20<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>3</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span> miles in 33 minutes 17 seconds; last photographed flight of the year</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Reporters showed up the next day (only their second appearance at the field since May the previous year), but the brothers declined to fly. The long flights convinced the Wrights they had achieved their goal of creating a flying machine of "practical utility" which they could offer to sell.</p>
<p>The only photos of the flights of 1904–1905 were taken by the brothers. (A few photos were damaged in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Dayton_Flood" title="Great Dayton Flood">Great Dayton Flood</a> of 1913, but most survived intact.) In 1904 Ohio beekeeping businessman <a href="/wiki/Amos_Root" title="Amos Root">Amos Root</a>, a technology enthusiast, saw a few flights including the first circle. Articles he wrote for his beekeeping magazine were the only published eyewitness reports of the Huffman Prairie flights, except for the unimpressive early hop local newsmen saw. Root offered a report to <i>Scientific American</i> magazine, but the editor turned it down. As a result, the news was not widely known outside of Ohio, and was often met with skepticism. The Paris edition of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Herald_Tribune" title="International Herald Tribune">Herald Tribune</a></i> headlined a 1906 article on the Wrights "FLYERS OR LIARS?"</p>
<p>In years to come Dayton newspapers would proudly celebrate the hometown Wright brothers as national heroes, but the local reporters somehow missed one of the most important stories in history as it was happening a few miles from their doorstep. <a href="/wiki/James_M._Cox" title="James M. Cox">James M. Cox</a>, publisher at that time of the <a href="/wiki/Dayton_Daily_News" title="Dayton Daily News">Dayton Daily News</a> (later governor of Ohio and Democratic presidential nominee in 1920), expressed the attitude of newspapermen—and the public—in those days when he admitted years later, "Frankly, none of us believed it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HeadlineWrightBros.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="195" data-file-width="401" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/HeadlineWrightBros.gif/220px-HeadlineWrightBros.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/HeadlineWrightBros.gif/330px-HeadlineWrightBros.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/HeadlineWrightBros.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HeadlineWrightBros.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i>Dayton Daily News</i> reported the October 5 flight on page 9, with agriculture and business news.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[N 1]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A few newspapers published articles about the long flights, but no reporters or photographers had been there. The lack of splashy eyewitness press coverage was a major reason for disbelief in Washington, D.C. and Europe and in journals like <i>Scientific American</i>, whose editors doubted the "alleged experiments" and asked how U.S. newspapers, "alert as they are, allowed these sensational performances to escape their notice."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October 1904 the brothers were visited by the first of many important Europeans they would befriend in coming years, <a href="/wiki/John_Capper" title="John Capper">Colonel J. E. Capper</a>, later superintendent of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Aircraft_Establishment" title="Royal Aircraft Establishment">Royal Balloon Factory</a>. Capper and his wife were visiting the United States to investigate the aeronautical exhibits at the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase_Exposition" title="Louisiana Purchase Exposition">St. Louis World Fair</a>, but had been given a letter of introduction to both Chanute and the Wrights by <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Young_Alexander" title="Patrick Young Alexander">Patrick Alexander</a>. Capper was very favorably impressed by the Wrights, who showed him photographs of their aircraft in flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Wright brothers were certainly complicit in the lack of attention they received. Fearful of competitors stealing their ideas, and still without a patent, they flew on only one more day after October 5. From then on, they refused to fly anywhere unless they had a firm contract to sell their aircraft. They wrote to the U.S. government, then to Britain, France and Germany with an offer to sell a flying machine, but were rebuffed because they insisted on a signed contract before giving a demonstration. They were unwilling even to show their photographs of the airborne Flyer. The American military, having recently spent $50,000 on the Langley Aerodrome—a product of the nation's foremost scientist—only to see it plunge twice into the Potomac River "like a handful of mortar", was particularly unreceptive to the claims of two unknown bicycle makers from Ohio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wb-smithson-langley_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-wb-smithson-langley-90">[89]</a></sup> Thus, doubted or scorned, the Wright brothers continued their work in semi-obscurity, while other aviation pioneers like Santos-Dumont, <a href="/wiki/Henri_Farman" title="Henri Farman">Henri Farman</a>, <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Delagrange" title="Léon Delagrange">Léon Delagrange</a> and American <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Curtiss" title="Glenn Curtiss">Glenn Curtiss</a> entered the limelight.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="European_skepticism">European skepticism</span></h2>
<p>In 1906 skeptics in the European aviation community had converted the press to an anti-Wright brothers stance. European newspapers, especially those in France, were openly derisive, calling them <i>bluffeurs</i> (bluffers).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EuroDoubt_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-EuroDoubt-91">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ernest_Archdeacon" title="Ernest Archdeacon">Ernest Archdeacon</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/A%C3%A9ro-Club_de_France" title="Aéro-Club de France">Aéro-Club de France</a>, was publicly scornful of the brothers' claims in spite of published reports; specifically, he wrote several articles and, in 1906, stated that "the French would make the first public demonstration of powered flight".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US_Cent_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-US_Cent-92">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Paris edition of the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Herald" title="New York Herald">New York Herald</a></i> summed up Europe's opinion of the Wright brothers in an editorial on February 10, 1906:</p>
<dl>
<dd>The Wrights have flown or they have not flown. They possess a machine or they do not possess one. They are in fact either fliers or liars. It is difficult to fly. It's easy to say, 'We have flown.'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EuroDoubt_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-EuroDoubt-91">[90]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>In 1908, after the Wrights' first flights in France, Archdeacon publicly admitted that he had done them an injustice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US_Cent_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-US_Cent-92">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contracts_and_return_to_Kitty_Hawk">Contracts and return to Kitty Hawk</span></h2>
<p>The Wright brothers made no flights at all in 1906 and 1907. They spent the time attempting to persuade the U.S. and European governments that they had invented a successful flying machine and were prepared to negotiate a contract to sell such machines. They also experimented with a pontoon and engine setup on the Miami River (Ohio) in hopes of flying from the water. These experiments proved unsuccessful.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1905_Wright_Flyer_Kill_Devil_Hills.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="513" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/1905_Wright_Flyer_Kill_Devil_Hills.jpg/220px-1905_Wright_Flyer_Kill_Devil_Hills.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/1905_Wright_Flyer_Kill_Devil_Hills.jpg/330px-1905_Wright_Flyer_Kill_Devil_Hills.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/1905_Wright_Flyer_Kill_Devil_Hills.jpg/440px-1905_Wright_Flyer_Kill_Devil_Hills.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1905_Wright_Flyer_Kill_Devil_Hills.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
