The modified 1905 Flyer at the Kill Devil Hills in 1908, ready for practice flights. Note there is no catapult derrick; all takeoffs were used with the monorail alone.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Replying to the Wrights' letters, the U.S. military expressed virtually no interest in their claims. The brothers turned their attention to Europe, especially France, where enthusiasm for aviation ran high, and journeyed there for the first time in 1907 for face-to-face talks with government officials and businessmen. They also met with aviation representatives in Germany and Britain. Before traveling, Orville shipped a newly built <a href="/wiki/Wright_Model_A" title="Wright Model A">Model A</a> Flyer to France in anticipation of demonstration flights.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:O.Wright_Soaring_1911.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="664" data-file-width="997" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/O.Wright_Soaring_1911.jpg/220px-O.Wright_Soaring_1911.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/O.Wright_Soaring_1911.jpg/330px-O.Wright_Soaring_1911.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/O.Wright_Soaring_1911.jpg/440px-O.Wright_Soaring_1911.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:O.Wright_Soaring_1911.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Soaring flight, Kitty Hawk, Oct., 1911 "Arrows indicate 50-Mile Wind, Showing How Machine Was Sustained in a Stationary Position"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wb-popmechnic-1911_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-wb-popmechnic-1911-93">[92]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In France Wilbur met <a href="/wiki/Frank_P._Lahm" title="Frank P. Lahm">Frank P. Lahm</a>, a lieutenant in the U.S. Army <a href="/wiki/Aeronautical_Division,_U.S._Signal_Corps" title="Aeronautical Division, U.S. Signal Corps">Aeronautical Division</a>. Writing to his superiors, Lahm smoothed the way for Wilbur to give an in-person presentation to the U.S. Board of Ordnance and Fortification in Washington, D.C. when he returned to the U.S. This time, the Board was favorably impressed, in contrast to its previous indifference. With further input from the Wrights, the <a href="/wiki/Signal_Corps_(United_States_Army)" title="Signal Corps (United States Army)">U.S. Army Signal Corps</a> issued Specification #486 in December 1907, inviting bids for construction of a flying machine under military contract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup> The Wrights submitted their bid in January.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[N 2]</a></sup> In early 1908 the brothers also agreed to a contract with a French company. In May they went back to Kitty Hawk with their 1905 Flyer to practice in private for their all-important public demonstration flights, as required by both contracts. Their privacy was lost when New York newspapers heard about the tests and sent several reporters to the scene.</p>
<p>Their contracts required them to fly with a passenger, so they modified the 1905 Flyer by installing two seats and adding upright control levers. After tests with sandbags in the passenger seat, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Furnas" title="Charles Furnas">Charlie Furnas</a>, a helper from Dayton, became the first fixed-wing aircraft passenger on a few short flights May 14, 1908. For safety, and as a promise to their father, Wilbur and Orville did not fly together. However, several newspaper accounts at the time mistakenly took Orville's flight with Furnas as both brothers flying together. Later that day after flying solo seven minutes, Wilbur suffered his worst crash when—still not well-acquainted with the two new control levers—he apparently moved one the wrong way and slammed the Flyer into the sand at between 40 and 50 miles per hour (64 and 80 km/h). He emerged with only bruises and a cut nose, but the accident ended the practice flights—and the airplane's flying career.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_glider_flights">Return to glider flights</span></h3>
<p>In October 1911, Orville Wright returned to the Outer Banks again, to conduct safety and stabilization tests with <a href="/wiki/Wright_Glider" title="Wright Glider">a new glider</a>. On October 24, he soared for nine minutes and 45 seconds, a record that held for almost 10 years, when <a href="/wiki/Gliding" title="Gliding">gliding</a> as a sport began in the 1920s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wb-fff-2011_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-wb-fff-2011-96">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_showing">Public showing</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wright-Fort_Myer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2190" data-file-width="2680" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Wright-Fort_Myer.jpg/220px-Wright-Fort_Myer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Wright-Fort_Myer.jpg/330px-Wright-Fort_Myer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Wright-Fort_Myer.jpg/440px-Wright-Fort_Myer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wright-Fort_Myer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Orville demonstrating the flyer to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army" title="U.S. Army">U.S. Army</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_Myer,_Virginia" title="Fort Myer, Virginia">Fort Myer</a>, Virginia September 1908. Photo: by C.H. Claudy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HartBerg_with_WilburWright.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="328" data-file-width="255" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/HartBerg_with_WilburWright.jpg/220px-HartBerg_with_WilburWright.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/ff/HartBerg_with_WilburWright.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HartBerg_with_WilburWright.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hart O. Berg (left), the Wrights' European business agent, and Wilbur at the flying field near Le Mans.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The brothers' contracts with the U.S. Army and a French syndicate depended on successful public flight demonstrations that met certain conditions. The brothers had to divide their efforts. Wilbur sailed for Europe; Orville would fly near Washington, D.C.</p>
<p>Facing much skepticism in the French aeronautical community and outright scorn by some newspapers that called him a "bluffeur", Wilbur began official public demonstrations on August 8, 1908 at the Hunaudières horse racing track near the town of <a href="/wiki/Le_Mans" title="Le Mans">Le Mans</a>, France. His first flight lasted only one minute 45 seconds, but his ability to effortlessly make banking turns and fly a circle amazed and stunned onlookers, including several pioneer French aviators, among them <a href="/wiki/Louis_Bl%C3%A9riot" title="Louis Blériot">Louis Blériot</a>. In the following days, Wilbur made a series of technically challenging flights, including figure-eights, demonstrating his skills as a pilot and the capability of his flying machine, which far surpassed those of all other pioneering aircraft and pilots of the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>The French public was thrilled by Wilbur's feats and flocked to the field by the thousands, and the Wright brothers instantly became world-famous. Former doubters issued apologies and effusive praise. <i><a href="/wiki/L%27A%C3%A9rophile" title="L'Aérophile">L'Aérophile</a></i> editor Georges Besançon wrote that the flights "have completely dissipated all doubts. Not one of the former detractors of the Wrights dare question, today, the previous experiments of the men who were truly the first to fly ..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup> Leading French aviation promoter Ernest Archdeacon wrote, "For a long time, the Wright brothers have been accused in Europe of bluff ... They are today hallowed in France, and I feel an intense pleasure ... to make amends."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>On October 7, 1908, Edith Berg, the wife of the brothers' European business agent, became the first American woman passenger when she flew with Wilbur—-one of many passengers who rode with him that autumn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[N 3]</a></sup> Wilbur also became acquainted with <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Boll%C3%A9e" title="Léon Bollée">Léon Bollée</a> and his family. Bollée was the owner of an automobile factory where Wilbur would assemble the Flyer and where he would be provided with hired assistance. Bollée would fly that autumn with Wilbur. Madame Bollée had been in the latter stages of pregnancy when Wilbur arrived in LeMans in June 1908 to assemble the Flyer. Wilbur promised her that he would make his first European flight the day her baby was born which he did, August 8, 1908.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Orville followed his brother's success by demonstrating another nearly identical Flyer to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a> at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Myer" title="Fort Myer">Fort Myer</a>, Virginia, starting on September 3, 1908. On September 9, he made the first hour-long flight, lasting 62 minutes and 15 seconds.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fort_Myer_Wright_Flyer_crash.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="450" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Fort_Myer_Wright_Flyer_crash.jpg/220px-Fort_Myer_Wright_Flyer_crash.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Fort_Myer_Wright_Flyer_crash.jpg/330px-Fort_Myer_Wright_Flyer_crash.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Fort_Myer_Wright_Flyer_crash.jpg/440px-Fort_Myer_Wright_Flyer_crash.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fort_Myer_Wright_Flyer_crash.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fort Myer crash. Photo by C.H. Claudy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wikisource-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="38"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original text related to this article:
<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Fatal_fall_of_Wright_airship" title="wikisource:Fatal fall of Wright airship">Fatal fall of Wright airship</a></b></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>On September 17, Army lieutenant <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Selfridge" title="Thomas Selfridge">Thomas Selfridge</a> rode along as his passenger, serving as an official observer. A few minutes into the flight at an altitude of about 100 feet (30 m), a propeller split and shattered, sending the Flyer out of control. Selfridge suffered a fractured skull in the crash and died that evening in the nearby Army hospital, becoming the first airplane crash fatality. Orville was badly injured, suffering a broken left leg and four broken ribs. Twelve years later, after he suffered increasingly severe pains, X-rays revealed the accident had also caused three hip bone fractures and a dislocated hip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup> The brothers' sister Katharine, a school teacher, rushed from Dayton to Virginia and stayed by Orville's side for the seven weeks of his hospitalization. She helped negotiate a one-year extension of the Army contract. A friend visiting Orville in the hospital asked, "Has it got your nerve?" "Nerve?" repeated Orville, slightly puzzled. "Oh, do you mean will I be <i>afraid</i> to fly again? The only thing I'm afraid of is that I can't get well soon enough to finish those tests next year."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>Deeply shocked and upset by the accident, Wilbur determined to make even more impressive flight demonstrations; in the ensuing days and weeks he set new records for altitude and duration. In January 1909 Orville and Katharine joined him in France, and for a time they were the three most famous people in the world, sought after by royalty, the rich, reporters and the public. The kings of Great Britain, Spain and Italy came to see Wilbur fly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[103]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1909_Flyer_and_Derrick.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="465" data-file-width="640" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/1909_Flyer_and_Derrick.jpg/220px-1909_Flyer_and_Derrick.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/1909_Flyer_and_Derrick.jpg/330px-1909_Flyer_and_Derrick.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/1909_Flyer_and_Derrick.jpg/440px-1909_Flyer_and_Derrick.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1909_Flyer_and_Derrick.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wright Model A Flyer flown by Wilbur 1908–1909 and launching derrick, France, 1909</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Wrights traveled to <a href="/wiki/Pau,_Pyr%C3%A9n%C3%A9es-Atlantiques" title="Pau, Pyrénées-Atlantiques">Pau</a>, in the south of France, where Wilbur made many more public flights, giving rides to a procession of officers, journalists and statesmen—and his sister Katharine on February 15. He trained two French pilots, then transferred the airplane to the French company. In April the Wrights went to Italy where Wilbur assembled another Flyer, giving demonstrations and training more pilots. An Italian cameraman <a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federico_Valle" title="fr:Federico Valle">Federico Valle</a> climbed aboard and filmed the first motion picture from an airplane.</p>
<p>After their return to the U.S., the brothers and Katharine were invited to the White House where <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">President Taft</a> bestowed awards upon them. Dayton followed up with a lavish two-day homecoming celebration. In July 1909 Orville, with Wilbur assisting, completed the proving flights for the U.S. Army, meeting the requirements of a two-seater able to fly with a passenger for an hour at an average of speed of 40 miles an hour (64 km/h) and land undamaged. They sold the airplane to the Army's Aeronautical Division, U.S. Signal Corps for $30,000 (which included a $5,000 bonus for exceeding the speed specification). Wilbur climaxed an extraordinary year in early October when he flew at New York City's Hudson-Fulton celebrations, circling the <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Liberty" title="Statue of Liberty">Statue of Liberty</a> and making a 33-minute flight up and down the Hudson River alongside Manhattan in view of up to one million New Yorkers. These flights solidly established the fame of the Wright brothers in America.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_flights">Family flights</span></h3>
<p>On May 25, 1910, back at Huffman Prairie, Orville piloted two unique flights. First, he took off on a six-minute flight with Wilbur as his passenger, the only time the Wright brothers ever flew together. They received permission from their father to make the flight. They had always promised Milton they would never fly together to avoid the chance of a double tragedy and to ensure one brother would remain to continue their experiments. Next, Orville took his 82-year-old father on a nearly seven-minute flight, the only one of Milton Wright's life. The aircraft rose to about 350 feet (107 m) while the elderly Wright called to his son, "Higher, Orville, higher!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Patent_war">Patent war</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Wright_brothers_patent_war" title="Wright brothers patent war">Wright brothers patent war</a></div>
<p>The Wright brothers wrote their 1903 <a href="/wiki/Patent_application" title="Patent application">patent application</a> themselves, but it was rejected. In January 1904 they hired Ohio patent attorney Henry Toulmin, and on May 22, 1906, they were granted U.S. Patent 821393<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flying_Machine_patent_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Flying_Machine_patent-13">[13]</a></sup> for "new and useful Improvements in Flying Machines".</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WrightPatentIntro.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="438" data-file-width="580" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/WrightPatentIntro.jpg/220px-WrightPatentIntro.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/WrightPatentIntro.jpg/330px-WrightPatentIntro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/WrightPatentIntro.jpg/440px-WrightPatentIntro.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WrightPatentIntro.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Patent_and_Trademark_Office" title="U.S. Patent and Trademark Office">U.S. Patent and Trademark Office</a> archive</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The patent illustrates a non-powered flying machine—namely, the 1902 glider. The patent's importance lies in its claim of a new and useful method of <i>controlling</i> a flying machine, powered or not. The technique of wing-warping is described, but the patent explicitly states that other methods instead of wing-warping could be used for adjusting the outer portions of a machine's wings to different angles on the right and left sides to achieve lateral (roll) control. The concept of varying the angle presented to the air near the wingtips, by any suitable method, is central to the patent. The patent also describes the steerable rear vertical rudder and its innovative use in combination with wing-warping, enabling the airplane to make a <i>coordinated turn</i>, a technique that prevents hazardous <i><a href="/wiki/Adverse_yaw" title="Adverse yaw">adverse yaw</a></i>, the problem Wilbur had when trying to turn the 1901 glider. Finally, the patent describes the forward elevator, used for ascending and descending.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lawsuits_begin">Lawsuits begin</span></h3>
<p>Attempting to circumvent the patent, Glenn Curtiss and other early aviators devised <a href="/wiki/Aileron" title="Aileron">ailerons</a> to emulate lateral control described in the patent and demonstrated by the Wrights in their public flights. Soon after the historic July 4, 1908, one-kilometer flight by Curtiss in the <a href="/wiki/AEA_June_Bug" title="AEA June Bug">AEA June Bug</a>, the Wrights warned him not to infringe their patent by profiting from flying or selling aircraft that used ailerons.</p>
<p>Curtiss was at the time a member of the <a href="/wiki/Aerial_Experiment_Association" title="Aerial Experiment Association">Aerial Experiment Association</a> (AEA), headed by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell" title="Alexander Graham Bell">Alexander Graham Bell</a>, where in 1908 he had helped reinvent wingtip ailerons for their Aerodrome No. 2, known as the <a href="/wiki/AEA_White_Wing" title="AEA White Wing">AEA White Wing</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aerospaceweb_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aerospaceweb-108">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Casey_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Casey-109">[106]</a></sup> (the AEA's other members became dismayed when Curtiss unexpectedly dropped out of their organization; they later came to believe he had sold the rights to their joint innovation to the United States Government).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Curtiss refused to pay license fees to the Wrights and sold an airplane equipped with ailerons to the Aeronautic Society of New York in 1909. The Wrights filed a lawsuit, beginning a years-long legal conflict. They also sued foreign aviators who flew at U.S. exhibitions, including the leading French aviator <a href="/wiki/Louis_Paulhan" title="Louis Paulhan">Louis Paulhan</a>. The Curtiss people derisively suggested that if someone jumped in the air and waved his arms, the Wrights would sue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>European companies which bought foreign patents the Wrights had received sued other manufacturers in their countries. Those lawsuits were only partly successful. Despite a pro-Wright ruling in France, legal maneuvering dragged on until the patent expired in 1917. A German court ruled the patent not valid because of prior disclosure in speeches by Wilbur Wright in 1901 and Chanute in 1903. In the U.S. the Wrights made an agreement with the <a href="/wiki/Aero_Club_of_America" title="Aero Club of America">Aero Club of America</a> to license airshows which the Club approved, freeing participating pilots from a legal threat. Promoters of approved shows paid fees to the Wrights. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[108]</a></sup> The Wright brothers won their initial case against Curtiss in February 1913 when a judge ruled that ailerons were covered under the patent. The Curtiss company appealed the decision.</p>
<p>From 1910 until his death from <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a> in 1912, Wilbur took the leading role in the patent struggle, traveling incessantly to consult with lawyers and testify in what he felt was a moral cause, particularly against Curtiss, who was creating a large company to manufacture aircraft. The Wrights' preoccupation with the legal issue stifled their work on new designs, and by 1911 Wright airplanes were considered inferior to those of European makers. Indeed, aviation development in the U.S. was suppressed to such an extent that when the U.S. entered World War I no acceptable American-designed airplanes were available, and U.S. forces were compelled to use French machines. Orville and Katharine Wright believed Curtiss was partly responsible for Wilbur's premature death, which occurred in the wake of his exhausting travels and the stress of the legal battle.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Victory_and_cooperation">Victory and cooperation</span></h3>
<p>In January 1914, a U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals upheld the verdict against the Curtiss company, which continued to avoid penalties through legal tactics. Orville apparently felt vindicated by the decision, and much to the frustration of company executives, he did not push vigorously for further legal action to ensure a manufacturing monopoly. In fact, he was planning to sell the company and departed in 1915. In 1917, with World War I underway, the U.S. government pressured the industry to form a cross-licensing organization, the Manufacturers Aircraft Association, to which member companies paid a blanket fee for the use of aviation patents, including the original and subsequent Wright patents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wb-centflight_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-wb-centflight-112">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[N 4]</a></sup> The Wright-Martin company (successor to the Wright company) and the Curtiss company (which held a number of its own patents) each received a $2 million payment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[N 5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[111]</a></sup> The "<a href="/wiki/Patent_war" title="Patent war">patent war</a>" ended, although side issues lingered in the courts until the 1920s. In a twist of irony, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wright_Aeronautical_Corporation" title="Wright Aeronautical Corporation">Wright Aeronautical Corporation</a> (another successor) and the Curtiss Aeroplane company merged in 1929 to form the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curtiss-Wright_Corporation" title="Curtiss-Wright Corporation">Curtiss-Wright Corporation</a>, which remains in business today producing high-tech components for the aerospace industry.</p>
<p>Aviation historian <a href="/wiki/Charles_Harvard_Gibbs-Smith" title="Charles Harvard Gibbs-Smith">Charles Harvard Gibbs-Smith</a> stated a number of times<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flight-1960.09.16_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flight-1960.09.16-118">[113]</a></sup> that the Wrights' legal victory would have been "doubtful" if an 1868 patent of "a prior but lost invention" by <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Piers_Watt_Boulton" title="Matthew Piers Watt Boulton">Matthew Piers Watt Boulton</a> of the UK had been known in the period 1903–1906.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aerospaceweb_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aerospaceweb-108">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbs-Smith_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbs-Smith-119">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[115]</a></sup> The patent, titled <i>Aërial Locomotion &amp;c,</i> described several engine improvements and conceptual designs and included a technical description and drawings of an <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_flight_control_system#Primary_controls" title="Aircraft flight control system">aileron control system</a> and an optional feature intended to function as an <a href="/wiki/Autopilot#Modern_autopilots" title="Autopilot">autopilot</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boulton_Patent.2C_No._392.2C_1868_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boulton_Patent.2C_No._392.2C_1868-121">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bridgements_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bridgements-122">[117]</a></sup> In fact, this patent was well-known to participants in the Wright-Curtiss lawsuit. A U.S. federal judge who reviewed previous inventions and patents and upheld the Wright patent against the Curtiss company reached the opposite conclusion of Gibbs-Smith, saying the Boulton patent "is not anticipatory".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_reactions">Public reactions</span></h3>
<p>The lawsuits damaged the public image of the Wright brothers, who were generally regarded before this as heroes. Critics said the brothers were greedy and unfair and compared their actions unfavorably to European inventors, who worked more openly. Supporters said the brothers were protecting their interests and were justified in expecting fair compensation for the years of work leading to their successful invention. Their 10-year friendship with Octave Chanute, already strained by tension over how much credit, if any, he might deserve for their success, collapsed after he publicly criticized their actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_business">In business</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wright_Brothers_in_1910.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="699" data-file-width="565" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Wright_Brothers_in_1910.jpg/220px-Wright_Brothers_in_1910.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Wright_Brothers_in_1910.jpg/330px-Wright_Brothers_in_1910.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Wright_Brothers_in_1910.jpg/440px-Wright_Brothers_in_1910.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wright_Brothers_in_1910.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wright brothers at the <a href="/wiki/Belmont_Park" title="Belmont Park">Belmont Park</a> Aviation Meet in 1910</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Wright_Company" title="Wright Company">Wright Company</a> was incorporated on November 22, 1909. The brothers sold their patents to the company for $100,000 and also received one-third of the shares in a million dollar stock issue and a 10 percent royalty on every airplane sold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[120]</a></sup> With Wilbur as president and Orville as vice president, the company set up a factory in Dayton and a <a href="/wiki/Wright_Flying_School" title="Wright Flying School">flying school</a>/test flight field at Huffman Prairie; the headquarters office was in New York City.</p>
<p>In mid-1910, the Wrights changed the design of the Wright Flyer, moving the horizontal elevator from the front to the back and adding wheels although keeping the skids as part of the undercarriage unit. It had become apparent by then that a rear elevator would make an airplane easier to control, especially as higher speeds grew more common. The new version was designated the "Model B", although the original canard design was never referred to as the "Model A" by the Wrights. However, the U.S. Army Signal Corps which bought the airplane did call it "Wright Type A".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[N 6]</a></sup></p>
<p>There were not many customers for airplanes, so in the spring of 1910 the Wrights hired and trained a <a href="/wiki/Wright_Exhibition_Team" title="Wright Exhibition Team">team</a> of salaried exhibition pilots to show off their machines and win prize money for the company—despite Wilbur's disdain for what he called "the mountebank business". The team debuted at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indianapolis_Speedway" title="Indianapolis Speedway">Indianapolis Speedway</a> on June 13. Before the year was over, pilots <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Johnstone" title="Ralph Johnstone">Ralph Johnstone</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arch_Hoxsey" title="Arch Hoxsey">Arch Hoxsey</a> died in air show crashes, and in November 1911 the brothers disbanded the team on which nine men had served (four other former team members died in crashes afterward).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Wright Company transported the first known commercial air cargo on November 7, 1910 by flying two bolts of dress silk 65 miles (105 km) from Dayton to Columbus, Ohio for the Morehouse-Martens Department Store, which paid a $5,000 fee. Company pilot <a href="/wiki/Philip_Orin_Parmelee" title="Philip Orin Parmelee">Phil Parmelee</a> made the flight—which was more an exercise in advertising than a simple delivery—in an hour and six minutes with the cargo strapped in the passenger's seat. The silk was cut into small pieces and sold as souvenirs.</p>
<p>Between 1910 and 1916 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wright_Brothers_Flying_School" title="Wright Brothers Flying School">Wright Brothers Flying School</a> at Huffman Prairie trained 115 pilots who were instructed by Orville and his assistants. Several trainees became famous, including <a href="/wiki/Henry_H._Arnold" title="Henry H. Arnold">Henry "Hap" Arnold</a>, who rose to Five-Star General, commanded U.S. Army Air Forces in World War II, and became first head of the U.S. Air Force; <a href="/wiki/Calbraith_Perry_Rodgers" title="Calbraith Perry Rodgers">Calbraith Perry Rodgers</a>, who made the first coast-to-coast flight in 1911 (with many stops and crashes) in a Wright Model EX named the "<a href="/wiki/Vin_Fiz_Flyer" title="Vin Fiz Flyer">Vin Fiz</a>" after the sponsor's soft drink; and Eddie Stinson, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Stinson_Aircraft_Company" title="Stinson Aircraft Company">Stinson Aircraft Company</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Army_accidents">Army accidents</span></h3>
<p>In 1912–1913 a series of fatal crashes of Wright airplanes bought by the U.S. Army called into question their safety and design. The death toll reached 11 by 1913, half of them in the Wright model C. All six model C Army airplanes crashed. They had a tendency to nose dive,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIL_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIL-129">[123]</a></sup> but Orville insisted that stalls were caused by pilot error.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[124]</a></sup> He cooperated with the Army to equip the airplanes with a rudimentary flight indicator to help the pilot avoid climbing too steeply. A government investigation said the Wright C was "dynamically unsuited for flying",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIL_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-MIL-129">[123]</a></sup> and the American military ended its use of airplanes with "pusher" type propellers, including models made by both the Wright and Curtiss companies, in which the engine was located behind the pilot and likely to crush him in a crash. Orville resisted the switch to manufacturing "<a href="/wiki/Tractor_configuration" title="Tractor configuration">tractor</a>"-type propeller aircraft, worried that a design change could threaten the Wright patent infringement case against Curtiss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Smithsonian_feud">Smithsonian feud</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LangleyAerodromeFlown.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="948" data-file-width="1658" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/LangleyAerodromeFlown.jpg/220px-LangleyAerodromeFlown.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/LangleyAerodromeFlown.jpg/330px-LangleyAerodromeFlown.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/LangleyAerodromeFlown.jpg/440px-LangleyAerodromeFlown.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LangleyAerodromeFlown.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Elwood Doherty, a Curtiss pilot,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[126]</a></sup> coaxes the structurally modified <a href="/wiki/Langley_Aerodrome" title="Langley Aerodrome">Langley Aerodrome</a> into the air above the surface of <a href="/wiki/Keuka_Lake" title="Keuka Lake">Keuka Lake</a> near Hammondsport, New York, September 17, 1914.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samuel_P._Langley" title="Samuel P. Langley">Samuel P. Langley</a>, secretary of the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a> from 1887 until his death in 1906, experimented for years with model flying machines and successfully flew unmanned powered fixed-wing model aircraft in 1896 and 1903. Two tests of his manned full-size motor-driven Aerodrome in October and December 1903, however, were complete failures. Nevertheless, the Smithsonian later proudly displayed the Aerodrome in its museum as the first heavier-than-air craft "capable" of manned powered flight, relegating the Wright brothers' invention to secondary status and triggering a decades-long feud with Orville Wright, whose brother had received help from the Smithsonian when beginning his own quest for flight. (Ironically, the Wright brothers were the initial recipients of the <a href="/wiki/Langley_Gold_Medal" title="Langley Gold Medal">Samuel P. Langley Medal for Aerodromics</a> from the Smithsonian in 1910.)</p>
<p>The Smithsonian based its claim for the Aerodrome on short test flights Glenn Curtiss and his team made with it in 1914. The Smithsonian allowed Curtiss, in an unsavory alliance, to make major modifications to the craft before attempting to fly it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[N 7]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Smithsonian hoped to salvage Langley's aeronautical reputation by proving the Aerodrome could fly; Curtiss wanted to prove the same thing to defeat the Wrights' patent lawsuits against him. The tests had no effect on the patent battle, but the Smithsonian made the most of them, honoring the Aerodrome in its museum and publications. The Institution did not reveal the extensive Curtiss modifications, but Orville Wright learned of them from his brother Lorin and a close friend of his and Wilbur's, Griffith Brewer, who both witnessed and photographed some of the tests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[128]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wright_flyer_-_full.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2436" data-file-width="4694" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Wright_flyer_-_full.jpg/220px-Wright_flyer_-_full.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Wright_flyer_-_full.jpg/330px-Wright_flyer_-_full.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Wright_flyer_-_full.jpg/440px-Wright_flyer_-_full.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wright_flyer_-_full.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Original 1903 Wright Flyer in the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a> in Washington, D.C.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Orville repeatedly objected to misrepresentation of the Aerodrome, but the Smithsonian was unyielding. Orville responded by lending the restored 1903 Kitty Hawk Flyer to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London_Science_Museum" title="London Science Museum">London Science Museum</a> in 1928, refusing to donate it to the Smithsonian while the Institution "perverted" the history of the flying machine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[129]</a></sup> Orville would never see his invention again as he would die before its return to the United States. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lindbergh" title="Charles Lindbergh">Charles Lindbergh</a> attempted to mediate the dispute, to no avail. In 1942, after years of bad publicity, and encouraged by Wright biographer <a href="/wiki/Fred_C._Kelly" title="Fred C. Kelly">Fred C. Kelly</a>, the Smithsonian finally relented by publishing, for the first time, a list of the Aerodrome modifications and recanting misleading statements it had made about the 1914 tests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wb-nps-abbot_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-wb-nps-abbot-137">[130]</a></sup> Orville then privately requested the British museum to return the Flyer, but the airplane remained in protective storage for the duration of World War II and finally came home after Orville's death.</p>
<p>On November 23, 1948, the executors of Orville's estate signed an agreement for the Smithsonian to purchase the Flyer for one dollar. At the insistence of the executors, the agreement also included strict conditions for display of the airplane.</p>
<p>The agreement reads, in part, "Neither the Smithsonian Institution or its successors, nor any museum or other agency, bureau or facilities administered for the United States of America by the Smithsonian Institution or its successors shall publish or permit to be displayed a statement or label in connection with or in respect of any aircraft model or design of earlier date than the 1903 Wright Aeroplane, claiming in effect that such aircraft was capable of carrying a man under its own power in controlled flight."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[N 8]</a></sup> If this agreement is not fulfilled, the Flyer can be reclaimed by the heir of the Wright brothers. Some aviation buffs, particularly those who promote the legacy of <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Whitehead" title="Gustave Whitehead">Gustave Whitehead</a>, now accuse the Smithsonian of refusing to investigate claims of earlier flights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[133]</a></sup> After a ceremony in the Smithsonian museum, the Flyer went on public display on December 17, 1948, the 45th anniversary of the only day it was flown successfully. The Wright brothers' nephew Milton (Lorin's son), who had seen gliders and the Flyer under construction in the bicycle shop when he was a boy, gave a brief speech and formally transferred the airplane to the Smithsonian, which displayed it with the accompanying label:</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="margin:auto; text-align:center; width:85%; height:95px;">
<tr>
<td>The original Wright brothers aeroplane
<p>The world's first power-driven heavier-than-air machine in which man made free, controlled, and sustained flight<br/>
Invented and built by Wilbur and Orville Wright<br/>
Flown by them at Kitty Hawk, North Carolina December 17, 1903<br/>
By original scientific research the Wright brothers discovered the principles of human flight<br/>
As inventors, builders, and flyers they further developed the aeroplane, taught man to fly, and opened the era of aviation</p>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_years">Last years</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_pilots_certificate_back.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="419" data-file-width="640" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/US_pilots_certificate_back.jpg/220px-US_pilots_certificate_back.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/US_pilots_certificate_back.jpg/330px-US_pilots_certificate_back.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/US_pilots_certificate_back.jpg/440px-US_pilots_certificate_back.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_pilots_certificate_back.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The back of the <a href="/wiki/Pilot_certification_in_the_United_States" title="Pilot certification in the United States">US Airman Certificate</a> with a picture of the Wright brothers.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wilbur">Wilbur</span></h3>
<p>Neither brother married. Wilbur once quipped that he did not have time for both a wife and an airplane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[134]</a></sup> Following a brief training flight in Berlin he gave to a German pilot in June 1911, Wilbur never flew again. He gradually became occupied with business matters for the Wright Company and dealing with different lawsuits. Upon dealing with the patent lawsuits, which had put great strain on both brothers, Wilbur had written in a letter to a French friend, "When we think what we might have accomplished if we had been able to devote this time to experiments, we feel very sad, but it is always easier to deal with things than with men, and no one can direct his life entirely as he would choose." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[135]</a></sup> Wilbur spent the next year before his death traveling, where he spent a full six months in Europe attending to various business and legal matters. Wilbur urged American cities to emulate the European – particularly Parisian – philosophy of apportioning generous public space near every important public building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[136]</a></sup> He was also constantly back and forth between New York, Washington and Dayton. All of the stresses were taking a toll on Wilbur physically. Orville would remark that he would "come home white".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCollough_2015.2C_p._256_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCollough_2015.2C_p._256-145">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>It was decided by the family that a new and far grander house would be built, using the money that the Wrights had earned through their inventions and business. Called affectionately <a href="/wiki/Hawthorn_Hill" title="Hawthorn Hill">Hawthorn Hill</a>, building had begun in the Dayton suburb of <a href="/wiki/Oakwood,_Montgomery_County,_Ohio" title="Oakwood, Montgomery County, Ohio">Oakwood, Ohio</a>, while Wilbur was in Europe. Katharine and Orville oversaw the project in his absence. Wilbur's one known expression upon the design of the house was that he have a room and bathroom of his own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCollough_2015.2C_p._256_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-McCollough_2015.2C_p._256-145">[137]</a></sup> The brothers hired <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schenck_and_Williams" title="Schenck and Williams">Schenck and Williams</a>, an architectural firm, to design the house, along with input from both Wilbur and Orville. Wilbur did not live to see its completion in 1914.</p>
<p>He became ill on a business trip to Boston in April 1912,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[138]</a></sup> the illness sometimes attributed to eating bad shellfish at a banquet. After returning to Dayton in early May 1912, worn down in mind and body, he fell ill again and was diagnosed with typhoid fever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[139]</a></sup> He lingered on, his symptoms relapsing and remitting for many days. Wilbur died, at age 45, at the Wright family home on May 30.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[N 9]</a></sup> His father wrote about Wilbur in his diary:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"A short life, full of consequences. An unfailing intellect, imperturbable temper, great self-reliance and as great modesty, seeing the right clearly, pursuing it steadfastly, he lived and died."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[141]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orville">Orville</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orville_Wright-1928.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1122" data-file-width="816" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Orville_Wright-1928.jpg/170px-Orville_Wright-1928.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Orville_Wright-1928.jpg/255px-Orville_Wright-1928.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Orville_Wright-1928.jpg/340px-Orville_Wright-1928.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orville_Wright-1928.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>Orville Wright, 1928</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Orville succeeded to the presidency of the Wright Company upon Wilbur's death. Sharing Wilbur's distaste for business but not his brother's executive skills, Orville sold the company in 1915.</p>
<p>After 42 years living at their residence on 7 Hawthorn Street, Orville, Katharine and their father, Milton, moved to Hawthorn Hill in the spring of 1914. Milton died in his sleep on April 3, 1917, at the age of 88. Up until his death, Milton had been very active, preoccupied with reading, writing articles for religious publications and enjoying his morning walks. He had also marched in a Dayton Woman's Suffrage Parade, along with Orville and Katharine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>Orville made his last flight as a pilot in 1918 in a 1911 Model B. He retired from business and became an elder statesman of aviation, serving on various official boards and committees, including the National Advisory Committee for Aeronautics (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NACA" title="NACA">NACA</a>), predecessor agency to the National Aeronautics and Space Administration (<a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>) and Aeronautical Chamber of Commerce (ACCA), predecessor to the <a href="/wiki/Aerospace_Industries_Association" title="Aerospace Industries Association">Aerospace Industries Association</a> (AIA).</p>
<p>Katharine married Henry Haskell of Kansas City, a former Oberlin classmate, in 1926. Orville was furious and inconsolable, feeling he had been betrayed by Katharine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[143]</a></sup> He refused to attend the wedding or even communicate with her. He finally agreed to see her, apparently at Lorin's insistence, just before she died of pneumonia on March 3, 1929.</p>
<p>Orville Wright served NACA for 28 years. In 1930, he received the first Daniel Guggenheim Medal established in 1928 by the Daniel Guggenheim Fund for the Promotion of Aeronautics. In 1936, he was elected a member of the National Academy of Sciences.</p>
<p>On April 19, 1944, the second production <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Corporation" title="Lockheed Corporation">Lockheed</a> <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Constellation" title="Lockheed Constellation">Constellation</a>, piloted by <a href="/wiki/Howard_Hughes" title="Howard Hughes">Howard Hughes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trans_World_Airlines" title="Trans World Airlines">TWA</a> president <a href="/wiki/Jack_Frye" title="Jack Frye">Jack Frye</a>, flew from <a href="/wiki/Burbank,_California" title="Burbank, California">Burbank, California</a>, to Washington, D.C. in 6 hours and 57 minutes (2300 mi – 330.9 mph). On the return trip, the airliner stopped at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wright_Field" title="Wright Field">Wright Field</a> to give Orville Wright his last airplane flight, more than 40 years after his historic first flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[144]</a></sup> He may even have briefly handled the controls. He commented that the wingspan of the Constellation was longer than the distance of his first flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yenne_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-yenne-154">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>Orville's last major project was supervising the reclamation and preservation of the 1905 Wright Flyer III, which historians describe as the first practical airplane.</p>
<p>Orville expressed sadness in an interview years later about the death and destruction brought about by the bombers of World War II:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"We dared to hope we had invented something that would bring lasting peace to the earth. But we were wrong ... No, I don't have any regrets about my part in the invention of the airplane, though no one could deplore more than I do the destruction it has caused. I feel about the airplane much the same as I do in regard to fire. That is, I regret all the terrible damage caused by fire, but I think it is good for the human race that someone discovered how to start fires and that we have learned how to put fire to thousands of important uses."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[146]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Orville died on January 30, 1948, over 35 years after his brother, following his second heart attack, having lived from the horse-and-buggy age to the dawn of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supersonic" title="Supersonic">supersonic</a> flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[147]</a></sup> Both brothers are buried in the family plot at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woodland_Cemetery,_Dayton,_Ohio" title="Woodland Cemetery, Dayton, Ohio">Woodland Cemetery, Dayton, Ohio</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[N 10]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_T._Daniels" title="John T. Daniels">John T. Daniels</a>, the Coast Guardsman who took their famous first flight photo, died the day after Orville did.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Competing_claims">Competing claims</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Claims_to_the_first_powered_flight" title="Claims to the first powered flight">Claims to the first powered flight</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Early_flying_machines" title="Early flying machines">Early flying machines</a></div>
<p>First flight claims are made for <a href="/wiki/Cl%C3%A9ment_Ader" title="Clément Ader">Clément Ader</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Whitehead" title="Gustave Whitehead">Gustave Whitehead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Pearse" title="Richard Pearse">Richard Pearse</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Jatho" title="Karl Jatho">Karl Jatho</a> for their variously documented tests in years prior to and including 1903. Claims that the first true flight occurred after 1903 are made for <a href="/wiki/Traian_Vuia" title="Traian Vuia">Traian Vuia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alberto_Santos-Dumont" title="Alberto Santos-Dumont">Alberto Santos-Dumont</a>. Supporters of the post-Wright pioneers argue that techniques used by the Wright brothers disqualify them as first to make successful airplane flights. Those techniques were: a launch rail; skids instead of wheels; a headwind at takeoff; and a catapult after 1903. Supporters of the Wright brothers argue that proven, repeated, controlled, and sustained flights by the brothers entitle them to credit as inventors of the airplane, regardless of those techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[149]</a></sup> The late aviation historian Charles Harvard Gibbs-Smith was a supporter of the Wrights' claim to primacy in flight. He wrote that a barn door can be made to "fly" for a short distance if enough energy is applied to it; he determined that the very limited flight experiments of Ader, Vuia and others were "powered hops" instead of fully controlled flights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbs-Smith_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbs-Smith-119">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ohio.E2.80.93North_Carolina_coinage_rivalry">Ohio–North Carolina coinage rivalry</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OH_winner.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="189" data-file-width="195" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/OH_winner.gif/170px-OH_winner.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6e/OH_winner.gif 1.5x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OH_winner.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center><a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> <a href="/wiki/50_State_Quarters" title="50 State Quarters">50 State Quarter</a> features the 1905 <a href="/wiki/Wright_Flyer_III" title="Wright Flyer III">Wright Flyer III</a> built and flown in Ohio, in a famous photo from <a href="/wiki/Huffman_Prairie" title="Huffman Prairie">Huffman Prairie</a></center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2001_NC_Proof.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1055" data-file-width="1085" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/2001_NC_Proof.png/170px-2001_NC_Proof.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/2001_NC_Proof.png/255px-2001_NC_Proof.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/2001_NC_Proof.png/340px-2001_NC_Proof.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2001_NC_Proof.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center><a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a> <a href="/wiki/50_State_Quarters" title="50 State Quarters">50 State Quarter</a> features the famous first flight photo of the 1903 <a href="/wiki/Wright_Flyer" title="Wright Flyer">Wright Flyer</a> I at <a href="/wiki/Kitty_Hawk,_North_Carolina" title="Kitty Hawk, North Carolina">Kitty Hawk</a>, North Carolina</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The U.S. states of Ohio and North Carolina both take credit for the Wright brothers and their world-changing inventions—Ohio because the brothers developed and built their design in Dayton, and North Carolina because Kitty Hawk was the site of the Wrights' first powered flight. With a spirit of friendly rivalry, Ohio adopted the slogan "Birthplace of Aviation" (later "Birthplace of Aviation Pioneers", recognizing not only the Wrights, but also astronauts <a href="/wiki/John_Glenn" title="John Glenn">John Glenn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">Neil Armstrong</a>, both Ohio natives). The slogan appears on <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_registration_plates_of_Ohio" title="Vehicle registration plates of Ohio">Ohio license plates</a>. North Carolina uses the slogan "First In Flight" on its license plates.</p>
<p>The site of the first flights in North Carolina is preserved as Wright Brothers National Memorial, while their Ohio facilities are part of Dayton Aviation Heritage National Historical Park. As the positions of both states can be factually defended, and each played a significant role in the history of flight, neither state has an exclusive claim to the Wrights' accomplishment.</p>
<p>Notwithstanding the competition between those two states, in 1937 the Wrights' final bicycle shop and home were moved from Dayton to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenfield_Village" title="Greenfield Village">Greenfield Village</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dearborn,_Michigan" title="Dearborn, Michigan">Dearborn, Michigan</a>, where they remain.</p>
